The Verse of Retaliation and Brotherhood in Religion
Maturidi refutes the claim that brotherhood in the verse excludes grave sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse of retaliation (2:178) proves that grave sinners are still called believers because Allah calls them brothers in religion.

Core Idea
Scripture itself calls grave sinners believers and brothers in faith.

Maturidi begins by addressing an objection based on the verse of retaliation. The verse speaks of brotherhood among believers, and some argue that this brotherhood is absolute. Maturidi explains that the brotherhood is specifically in religion, as the verse explicitly calls them believers first and then brothers, showing the title remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse of retaliation quoted
Read the Arabic passage that discusses the verse. Notice how Maturidi interprets the naming of brotherhood.
2
Identify the key point about brotherhood
Maturidi argues that brotherhood is not absolute but restricted to religion, because the verse preserves the name of faith.
3
Understand the condition of reward
Maturidi shows that reward is sometimes tied to the absolute name of believer, sometimes to restricted descriptions, so a grave sinner can still be called believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في آية القصاص وما فيه من تسمية الأخوة : إن الله لم يُعدّ على الأخوة المطلقة ثوابًا ولا مدحا ، وإنما كان ذلك في الأخوة في الدين. فيقال له : قد سماهم مؤمنين في أول الآية، ثم أبقى لهم اسم الأخوة في آخرها ، ولا معنى سبق يحتمل صرف ذكر الأخوة إليه؛ ثبت أنه في الدين مع إبقاء اسم الإيمان وأما الثواب فقد شرط مرة باسم المطلق ومرة باسم المقيد؛ من ذلك قوله : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا . ثم قد يجوز عندك التحذير مع وجود القول وإن وعد عليه الثواب، فمثله المؤمن باسم الإطلاق. وقال : والذين آمنوا بالله ورسله أولئك هم الصديقون ﴾ [الحديد، ۱۹/٥٧] الآية ، وكذلك قوله : والذين آمنوا بالله ورسله ولم يفرقوا بين أحد منهم الآية، و[في] صاحب الكبيرة يقال : آمن بالله ورسله ولم يفرق بين أحد من رسله، ثم جائز في مثله التخويف والوعيد. وكذلك قال الله : وإن تك حسنةً يضاعفها لدنه أجرا عظيما ﴾ [النساء، ٤٠/٤]؛ وصاحب الكبيرة قد أتى بحسنة، ويستحق ويؤتِ من الذي جاء به اسم الحسنة ، فما تنكر أن يستحق اسم المؤمن وإن أوعد. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم عارض نفسه بالحقوق التي أوجبت باسم الإيمان وأُحلّت به وقد دخل في ذلك أصحاب الكبائر . فأجاب بأن إدخالهم بالإجماع لا بالاسم، كما أدخلتم في قوله: حقاً على المتقين ﴾ [البقرة، ۱۸۰/۲] و [حقاً على ] المحسنين ﴾ [البقرة، ٢٣٦/٢] وإن لم يكن الفاسق كذلك. قيل له : الإجماع أدخلهم في ذلك بالفهم من الخطاب بالإيجاب والتحليل [متمسكا] بالآيات، إذ ليس أحد منهم ذكر وجها به عرفوا سواه ، ولا أحد من متعاطى الفسق سأل / أحدًا عن خاص، بل عرف تضمنه تلك الآيات؛ ولم يجز الخطاب بالوجهين
And he said regarding the verse of retaliation and what it contains of naming brotherhood: Allah did not count reward or praise for absolute brotherhood, but that was for brotherhood in religion. So it is said to him: He called them believers at the beginning of the verse, then preserved for them the name of brotherhood at its end, and there is no prior meaning that could divert the mention of brotherhood to it; it is established that it is in religion while preserving the name of faith. As for reward, it is conditioned sometimes by the absolute name and sometimes by the restricted name; from that is His saying: 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said' (5:85). Then threat is possible in your view despite the existence of the statement, even if reward is promised for it; similarly, the believer is under the absolute name. And He said: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers, those are the truthful' (57:19) the verse, and likewise His saying: 'And those who believe in Allah and His messengers and make no distinction between any of them' (4:152) the verse, and concerning the committer of a major sin, it is said: he believed in Allah and His messengers and made no distinction between any of His messengers, then threat and warning are possible in such a case. And likewise Allah said: 'And if there is a good deed, He multiplies it and gives from Himself a great reward' (4:40); and the committer of a major sin has performed a good deed, and he deserves and receives the name of good deed from what he brought, so what prevents you from saying he deserves the name of believer even if he is threatened? And there is no power except with Allah. Then he countered himself with the rights that are obligated and made permissible in the name of faith, and the committers of major sins are included in that. So he answered that their inclusion is by consensus, not by name, just as you included them in His saying: 'As a duty upon the righteous' (2:180) and 'as a duty upon the doers of good' (2:236) even though the sinner is not like that. It was said to him: Consensus included them in that by understanding from the discourse of obligation and permissibility, holding to the verses, since none of them mentioned a way by which they knew otherwise, and none of those who practice sin asked anyone about a specific, but rather they knew the implication of those verses; and the discourse could not apply to both aspects.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of the Mu'tazilite position. First, it addresses the verse of retaliation (2:178) which calls believers brothers. The author argues that the brotherhood is in religion because the verse explicitly calls them believers at the start and brothers at the end, and no prior context diverts it. Rewards are sometimes tied to the absolute name 'believer' and sometimes to restricted descriptions. Then he cites verses about believers and the truthful (57:19) and those who believe without distinction (4:152), noting that a grave sinner can be described as believing in Allah and His messengers, yet still be subject to threats. He also cites 4:40, noting that a grave sinner does good deeds and receives the name 'good deed', so why not the name 'believer'? Finally, he addresses a self-objection: rights are given by the name 'believer' and include grave sinners. The opponent claims this is by consensus, not by the name. The author replies that consensus derives from understanding the verses directly, not from an external source, and that the discourse (Qur'an) cannot simultaneously apply to two different meanings (i.e., excluding and including grave sinners).

Key Terms
آية القصاص
أخوة
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the verse of retaliation indicate about the brotherhood of grave sinners?
A) It is absolute.
B) It is in religion.
C) It is conditional on repentance.
D) It excludes sinners.

Key Takeaway
The verse of retaliation uses the term brotherhood in the context of religion, confirming that grave sinners retain the title of believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Allah calls grave sinners 'brothers in faith.' Has your attitude toward a fellow Muslim who commits a major sin been affected by this? Will you now see them as a brother despite their fault, and how will you act on that?
?
Maturidi argues that the name of faith remains even if reward is conditioned. Do you treat your own faith as a stable identity or do you let your sins make you doubt it? What would change if you fully embraced that your iman is not negated by your mistakes?
?
The opponent in this passage tried to restrict brotherhood to the righteous. In your heart, do you ever secretly exclude some Muslims from the brotherhood of faith because of their actions? How will you correct this tendency starting today?

Scriptural Evidences for Believers Committing Major Sins
Maturidi provides additional scriptural evidence that grave sinners remain believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses verses about believers and good deeds to argue that grave sinners still deserve the name of believer.

Core Idea
A grave sinner who has faith and does good deeds is still a believer.

Maturidi continues his argument by citing several Qur'anic verses that describe believers. He shows that the same descriptions apply to grave sinners, and that even their good deeds merit the name of good deed, so why not the name of believer?

Guided Path
1
Examine the cited verses
Read the verses Maturidi quotes (57:19, 4:152) and note how he applies them to grave sinners.
2
Understand the parallel with good deeds
Notice the argument from 4:40: a grave sinner does good deeds and is called a doer of good, so why not a believer?
3
Apply the reasoning
The key is that the name of believer is based on faith, not on sinlessness.

The Role of Consensus in Including Grave Sinners
Maturidi refutes the claim that inclusion of grave sinners in Islamic rights is only by consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi responds to the claim that inclusion of grave sinners in Islamic rights is by consensus, arguing that consensus is derived directly from the Qur'anic texts.

Core Idea
Consensus includes grave sinners because it is based on understanding the Qur'an, not on an independent authority.

Maturidi addresses a self-objection: some argue that the inclusion of grave sinners in rights like inheritance is only by consensus, not by the name of believer. He responds that consensus itself derives from the direct understanding of the Qur'anic verses, and that the discourse cannot have two contradictory meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Maturidi raises the objection that inclusion of grave sinners in Islamic rights is by consensus, not by the name.
2
Understand his response
He argues that consensus is based on understanding the verses directly, not on an external source.
3
Note the final point
The discourse (Qur'an) cannot apply to two contradictory meanings; it must be consistent.

Source Text

?

Rights of Faith: By Name or by Consensus?
Maturidi answers the objection that major sinners share in rights of faith by consensus, not by the name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses consensus to explain why major sinners share in the rights of faith without bearing the name 'believer'.

Core Idea
Inclusion in faith's rights is by consensus, not by the name itself.

Maturidi anticipates an important objection: if rights like inheritance are tied to the name 'believer', then why do major sinners, whose actions contradict faith, share in these rights? He answers by distinguishing between inclusion by name and inclusion by consensus, drawing an analogy to Quranic phrases about piety. This passage clarifies the function of consensus in defining religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Notice the structure: Maturidi first states the objection that rights are linked to the name of faith, then gives his answer. Identify the two parts.
The objection is presented as a self-objection (عارض نفسه).
2
Understand the analogy
Examine the comparison with Quranic phrases like 'a right upon the pious' (Q 2:180). How does this analogy support Maturidi's response?
3
Analyze the role of consensus
Look at the argument that consensus includes major sinners by understanding the general discourse of obligation and permissibility, not by the name. No one proposed an alternative method.
Consensus is based on the understanding of the community, not on a specific textual application.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this reasoning protects the distinction between believers and major sinners while maintaining legal unity in rights.
The name 'believer' is not applied to major sinners, but they are included in rulings by consensus.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالحقوق التي أوجبت باسم الإيمان وأُحلّت به وقد دخل في ذلك أصحاب الكبائر . فأجاب بأن إدخالهم بالإجماع لا بالاسم، كما أدخلتم في قوله: حقاً على المتقين و حقاً على المحسنين وإن لم يكن الفاسق كذلك. قيل له : الإجماع أدخلهم في ذلك بالفهم من الخطاب بالإيجاب والتحليل متمسكا بالآيات، إذ ليس أحد منهم ذكر وجها به عرفوا سواه ، ولا أحد من متعاطى الفسق سأل أحدًا عن خاص، بل عرف تضمنه تلك الآيات؛ ولم يجز الخطاب بالوجهين باسم التقوى، لذلك بطل التقدير . وقوله : «حق على كذا ، أي حق على من يريد التقوى في ذلك وليس فيه إيجاب مع ما كان في الذي يُذكر تخصيص معنى التقوى في حق الخطاب ، وفيما نحن فيه يُدخله في الخطاب بلا اسمِ الذي به خوطب لا بالإطلاق ولا بالتخصيص ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he objected with the rights that are established by the name of faith and made permissible by it, and the committers of major sins have entered into that. So he answered that their inclusion is by consensus (ijmāʿ), not by the name, just as you included [them] in His saying: 'A right upon the pious' and '[a right] upon the doers of good' even though the sinner is not like that. It was said to him: Consensus included them in that by understanding from the discourse of obligation and making permissible, holding fast to the verses, since none of them mentioned a way by which they were known otherwise, nor did anyone who commits sin ask anyone about a specific [matter], but they recognized that those verses encompass them; and it is not permissible for the discourse to be on two faces with the name of piety, therefore the estimation is invalid. And His saying: 'a right upon such-and-such' means a right upon whoever intends piety in that, and there is no obligation in it, along with what there is in that which is mentioned of specifying the meaning of piety in the context of the discourse, and in our case it includes him in the discourse without the name by which he was addressed, neither absolutely nor specifically. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about whether the rights (huqūq) established by the name of faith apply to major sinners. The opponent objects that rights such as inheritance and marriage are established by the name 'believer' and major sinners share in them. Maturidi answers that their inclusion is by consensus (ijmāʿ), not by the name itself. He compares this to Quranic phrases like 'a right upon the pious' (Q 2:180, 236) where the name 'pious' is not applied to sinners, yet they may be included by consensus in certain rulings. The response clarifies that consensus operates by understanding from the divine discourse (khiṭāb) of obligation (ījāb) and making things permissible (taḥlīl), holding to the verses. The consensus is known because no one gave an alternative method for knowing these rulings, and no one among the sinners asked for a specific exception; they understood that the verses encompass them. Therefore, it is not permissible for the discourse to be on two different levels using the name 'piety' (taqwā). Hence, the opponent's estimation (taqdīr) that the name itself includes them fails. The phrase 'a right upon such-and-such' means a right upon whoever intends piety in that action, not an obligation, and there is a specification of the meaning of piety in the context. In the case of faith, the discourse includes the major sinner without using the name by which he was addressed, neither absolutely nor specifically. He ends with 'there is no power except by God.'

Misconception: Sharing in rights of faith implies that the name 'believer' applies equally to major sinners.
Correction: Maturidi shows that inclusion is by consensus, not by the name, preserving the distinction.

Refuting Objections: Minor, Insane, and the Sinner's Worship
Maturidi responds to two objections about the validity of a sinner's worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends the validity of a sinner's acts of worship against objections based on the cases of the minor and insane.

Core Idea
A sinner's worship is valid because of his faith, not in spite of his sin.

This passage presents two objections that might be raised against the view that a major sinner's acts of worship are valid and beneficial. The first objection compares the sinner to a minor or insane person who is only included under faith through others. The second questions why a sinner should pray and fast at all. Maturidi's refutations strengthen the principle that faith, even when accompanied by sin, grounds the validity of worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the literal translation. Note how the two objections are introduced and answered.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical sequence.
2
Understand the first objection and answer
Identify the objection about the insane and minor. Maturidi argues that their rulings depend on an external factor (guardian's faith), which does not apply to the sinner. The sinner's own faith is the basis.
3
Analyze the second objection and answer
Notice that Maturidi first gives a provisional answer (to prevent sin increase), then the Shaykh corrects a misunderstanding. The real point: the acts are valid because of faith; without faith they would be void.
This clarifies that faith is the underlying cause.
4
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider why this matters: It affirms that even a sinful believer remains a believer, and his worship is accepted, contrary to extremist views that nullify faith due to sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض في التحليل بالمجنون والصغير. قيل لهما حكم الإيمان بغيرهما، إذ لولا ذلك الغير لم يجب لهما ذلك، كما لم يجب لأولاد الكفرة. وما نحن فيه لا غير في ذلك يتبعه، فثبت أنه استوجبه بإيمان نفسه. ثم عورض بما يصلى الفاسق ويصوم ، فقال : لئلا يزداد فسقه، ويزول عنه عذاب تركهما . { قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } يقال له : لم تفهم السؤال، وإنما معنى ذلك أنهما يجوزان بالإيمان، فلولا أنه مؤمن لم يكن ليمضيا له ويزيلا عذاب تركهما عنه، بل لا شيء عليه في تركهما، ولا يجوز أن يفعله لو لم يكن مؤمنًا ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And he objected concerning making permissible by [the case of] the insane and the minor. It was said to them: The ruling of faith [applies] due to something other than them, for if not for that other, it would not be obligatory for them, just as it is not obligatory for the children of disbelievers. And in our case, there is no other thing in that which it follows, so it is established that he deserved it by his own faith. Then he was objected to with what the sinner prays and fasts, so he said: Lest his sin increase, and the punishment for abandoning them be removed from him. { The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: } It is said to him: You did not understand the question; rather the meaning of that is that they are rendered valid by faith, for if he were not a believer, they would not be valid for him and would not remove the punishment for abandoning them, rather there would be nothing upon him in abandoning them, and it would not be permissible for him to do them if he were not a believer. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and their answers. First, the opponent objected respecting the permissibility (taḥlīl) of certain acts by citing the cases of the insane (majnūn) and the minor (ṣaghīr), who may be included in rulings of faith through the faith of their guardians. Maturidi answers that these cases depend on something other than themselves (e.g., guardian's faith), but in the case of the major sinner, there is no such external factor; he deserves the rulings by his own faith. Second, the opponent objected by pointing out that the sinner prays and fasts, asking why these acts are valid if he is a sinner. Maturidi's initial response is that he does so to prevent his sin from increasing and to remove the punishment for abandoning them. Then the Shaykh (Maturidi himself or a commentator) intervenes, pointing out that the opponent misunderstood the question. The real meaning is that these acts (prayer and fasting) are rendered valid by faith; if he were not a believer, they would not be valid and would not remove the punishment for abandoning them. In fact, if he were not a believer, there would be no obligation on him to do them at all, and he could not even validly perform them. Thus, the sinner's ability to pray and fast actually proves he is a believer. The lesson ends with 'there is no power except by God.'

الصغير
الحكم
الإيمان
الصلاة
الصوم
الفسق
العذاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a major sinner's prayers and fasts are invalid or worthless because of his sin.
Correction: Maturidi argues that these acts are valid because of his underlying faith; they are accepted and bring benefit, even though sin remains.

Checkpoint

A) Because they prevent his sin from increasing.
B) Because he is a believer; without faith they would be void.
C) Because his guardian's faith covers him.
D) Because he can combine sin and worship.

Key Takeaway
A sinner's acts of worship are valid and beneficial because they stem from his faith, which remains intact despite sin.

Maturidi's Fifth Argument: Disbelief as a Permanent Disposition
Maturidi argues that disbelief is a fixed disposition, so its punishment is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that disbelief is a fixed disposition, which justifies its eternal punishment, and how this differs from temporary major sins.

Core Idea
Disbelief is a permanent state, so its punishment is forever.

Maturidi presents a fifth argument based on the nature of disbelief. He distinguishes disbelief from other major sins by their fundamental character: disbelief is a persistent disposition (madhhab ya‘qid) that becomes fixed, while other sins are momentary acts driven by fleeting desires. This distinction determines the nature of their respective punishments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the core distinction between disbelief and other sins.
Notice the contrast between 'madhhab ya‘qid' and 'tuf‘al li-l-awqāt'.
2
Identify the two categories
Disbelief is described as a permanent disposition (madhhab ya‘qid), while other major sins are described as actions done for moments (tuf‘al li-l-awqāt).
The word 'madhhab' implies a persistent school or position.
3
Understand the logical link
The punishment corresponds to the nature of the act: eternal for a permanent state, temporary for a temporary act.
Maturidi uses the principle that dispositions are fixed forever (tu‘qad li-l-abad).
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This argument reinforces the justice of eternal punishment for disbelief, distinguishing it from other sins that may be forgiven or punished temporarily.
This is a kalam argument based on the intrinsic nature (dhāt) of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوجه الآخر من طريق الاعتبار أن الكفر مذهب يعقد، والمذاهب تعقد للأبد، فعلى ذلك عقوبته؛ وسائر الكبائر تفعل للأوقات، وهو عند غلبة الشهوات، لا للأبد، فعلى ذلك عقوبتها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the other aspect from the way of consideration is that disbelief is a persistent disposition, and dispositions are fixed forever, so accordingly its punishment is eternal; while other major sins are done for moments, and they occur at times of overpowering desires, not forever, so accordingly their punishment is temporary. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth argument (introduced as 'al-wajh al-ākhar'). It distinguishes disbelief (kufr) from other major sins based on their nature. Disbelief is a 'persistent disposition' (madhhab ya‘qid)—a fixed, enduring state. Such dispositions are 'fixed forever' (tu‘qad li-l-abad), so their punishment must be eternal. In contrast, other major sins (sā’ir al-kabā’ir) are momentary actions ('tuf‘al li-l-awqāt') committed under the pressure of desires, not permanent. Therefore, their punishment is temporary, corresponding to their transient nature.

Key Terms

المذهب
الكبائر
العقوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equally permanent acts deserving eternal punishment.
Correction: The passage shows that disbelief is a permanent disposition, while other major sins are temporary, so their punishments differ accordingly.

Checkpoint

A) Because disbelief is a temporary act
B) Because disbelief is a fixed disposition
C) Because God wills it
D) Because it is a major sin

Key Takeaway
Disbelief's permanence justifies its eternal punishment, unlike temporary major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Disbelief Is Inherently Evil
Maturidi argues that disbelief is intrinsically evil and cannot be pardoned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers disbelief (kufr) to be inherently evil and thus unforgivable, while other sins may be pardoned.

Core Idea
Disbelief is evil by essence, so it can never become permissible.

This lesson presents a key argument from Imam Maturidi distinguishing disbelief from other sins. He claims that disbelief is evil in itself—not because of external factors—and therefore cannot be made permissible or forgiven. Other sins, however, are not inherently evil in this way, so their punishment can be lifted. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping Maturidi's theology of forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the contrast between 'kufr' and 'sā'ir al-ma'āthim' (other sins).
The phrase 'qabīḥ li-'aynihi' is the core concept.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi says disbelief does not admit of being set free or having its prohibition lifted. Other sins can have their prohibition lifted intellectually. Understand what this means: shirk is intrinsically wrong, so it can never become halal.
Contrast this with sins like drinking alcohol, which could theoretically be permitted by a new law.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does Maturidi say the punishment for disbelief cannot be lifted? Connect this to the idea that its evil is intrinsic, not circumstantial. This is why shirk is unforgivable if one dies upon it.
This argument supports the Islamic doctrine that Allah forgives all sins except shirk (associating partners).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الكفر قبيح لعينه، لا يحتمل الإطلاق ورفع الحرمة، فعلى ذلك عقوبته في الحكمة لا يحتمل الارتفاع والعفو عنه؛ وسائر المآثم جائز رفع الحرمة عنها في العقل وإباحة ما له [من] العقوبة، فمثله عقوبته. والله الموفق.
And the second: that disbelief is inherently evil, it does not admit of being set free or having its prohibition lifted, so accordingly its punishment in wisdom does not admit of being lifted or being forgiven; while other sins, it is permissible in the intellect to lift their prohibition and allow what [punishment] belongs to them, so their punishment is like that. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument (the 'second' in a series on page 457). It states that disbelief is 'inherently evil' (qabīḥ li-‘aynihi). Because of this intrinsic evil, it cannot be 'set free' (iṭlāq) or have its prohibition (ḥurma) lifted—meaning it can never become permissible. Consequently, its punishment cannot be lifted or forgiven. In contrast, other sins (sā’ir al-ma’āthim) are not inherently evil in the same way; their prohibition can be lifted in the intellect (ja’iz raf‘ al-ḥurma ‘anhā fī al-‘aql), and their punishment can be remitted. Therefore, the punishment for shirk is fixed, while other sins may be forgiven.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه
الإطلاق
رفع الحرمة
العفو
المآثم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equally inherently evil, so none can be forgiven.
Correction: Maturidi argues that only disbelief is inherently evil; other sins are not evil in themselves, so they can be forgiven.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is inherently evil and cannot be made permissible
B) Because God does not forgive any sin
C) Because it is a minor sin
D) Because the intellect cannot conceive of forgiveness

Key Takeaway
Disbelief is the only sin that is inherently evil, making it unforgivable if one dies upon it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that disbelief is inherently evil, or do you sometimes view it as a harmless difference of opinion? How will you actively cultivate hatred for kufr in your heart, while still showing compassion to those trapped in it?
?
This passage says that unlike other sins, disbelief cannot be forgiven. If you were to die tonight, are you certain your faith is genuine and free from any form of disbelief? What step will you take today to strengthen your iman and remove any doubt?
?
You know that shirk is the only unforgivable sin, but does that knowledge make you complacent about other sins, thinking they are trivial? How will you treat every sin as serious, knowing that persistence in sin can lead to a hardened heart and ultimately to disbelief?

Forgiveness of the Disbeliever Is Misplaced
Maturidi argues that forgiving a disbeliever is inappropriate because he denies the Benefactor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Maturidi, forgiveness of a disbeliever is inappropriate because he denies the Benefactor.

Core Idea
Forgiving one who denies the Giver wastes the gift.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the seventh argument against the possibility of forgiveness for disbelief. He argues that forgiveness is only meaningful when the recipient acknowledges the Benefactor; otherwise, it is wasted. The passage contrasts the disbeliever with other sinners who recognize God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the key ideas: forgiveness (العفو), the Benefactor (المنعم), and blessing (النعمة).
2
Identify the key contrast
Notice how Maturidi contrasts the disbeliever with other sinners. The disbeliever 'denies the Benefactor' while other sinners 'recognize the Benefactor'. This distinction is central to the argument.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for forgiveness? The commentary explains that forgiving the disbeliever would waste forgiveness and nullify the blessing. How does this relate to God's wisdom?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن العفو عن الكافر عفو في غير موضع العفو؛ لأنه منكر المنعم ويرى ذلك حقاً، فيكون في ذلك تضييع العفو وإبطال النعمة؛ ولا كذلك أمر سائر المآثم، بل يعرف صاحبها المنعم، فله أعظم الموضع، ولإكرامه أبين المحل، فجائز المغفرة له والعفو عنه في الحكمة. وبالله المعونة.
And the third: that forgiveness of the disbeliever is forgiveness in a place not appropriate for forgiveness, because he denies the Benefactor and considers that true, so in that there is a waste of forgiveness and an annulment of the blessing. And not so is the case of other sins; rather, their committer recognizes the Benefactor, so he has the greatest standing, and for his honor, the most evident position, so forgiveness and pardon for him are permissible in wisdom. And with Allah is the assistance.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument (the 'third' in this section). It addresses the appropriateness of forgiveness ('afw). Forgiving a disbeliever is described as 'forgiveness in a place not appropriate for forgiveness' because the disbeliever denies the Benefactor (al-mun‘im) and considers his denial true. This would waste forgiveness and nullify the blessing (ni‘ma). In contrast, the committer of other sins (sā’ir al-ma’āthim) recognizes the Benefactor, so he has the greatest standing (a‘zam al-mawḍi‘) and honor. Therefore, forgiveness for such a person is permissible in divine wisdom. Thus, shirk is not forgivable because it involves ingratitude and denial of the source of all blessings.

المنعم
النعمة
المغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God forgives everyone equally regardless of their recognition of Him.
Correction: Maturidi argues that forgiveness is appropriate only when the recipient acknowledges the Benefactor; the disbeliever denies Him, so forgiveness would be wasted.

Checkpoint

A) Because he denies the Benefactor
B) Because he commits major sins
C) Because he is not human
D) Because he does not ask for forgiveness

Key Takeaway
The disbeliever's denial of the Benefactor makes forgiveness inappropriate because it would nullify the blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This lesson shows that ingratitude to the point of denying the Giver is the ultimate barrier to forgiveness. Is there any area of your life where you are ungrateful? What specific blessing are you taking for granted today that, if you denied its Source, would cut you off from mercy?

Divine Favor: The Inherent Respect for God and His Prophets
Maturidi's eighth argument on divine favor and inherent respect for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that God's favor upon the believer includes an inherent respect for God and His messengers, which makes the believer deserving of forgiveness.

Core Idea
God's gift of veneration for His prophets is the believer's hidden treasure of grace.

Maturidi presents the eighth argument (the 'fourth' in this series) to demonstrate that faith is not merely a human achievement but a divine gift. He focuses on the profound respect that God places in the believer's heart for His own rights and for His prophets. This respect is a form of divine favor (iḥsān) that distinguishes the believer from the disbeliever and makes forgiveness possible. Let's explore this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument in a single flowing sentence.
The passage is dense; reading it aloud can help.
2
Identify the key components
Maturidi lists several aspects of divine favor: making God's right greater than the two worlds, exalting prophets, and preventing the believer from inclining to enemies. Identify each component in the text.
These components show how veneration is embedded in the believer's nature.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does this veneration make the believer eligible for forgiveness? Consider how this argument contrasts the believer with the disbeliever who lacks such respect.
This connects to the broader Maturidi defense of faith as a divine gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع أن يكون الله تعالى قد أحسن إليه في الدين في الوقت الذي جفاه هو بفعله: في أن جعل حقه أعظم في قلبه من الدارين، وأنبيائه ورسله أجلَّ في صدره من أن تحتمل نفسه الاستخفاف بشعرة من شعورهم أو الاستهانة بشيء من أمور دين الله أو الركون إلى من أعدائه فيها قد اختاره وآثره [بدلاً] من الخلان الله، وكل ذلك هو إحسان الله إليه
And the fourth: that Allah, the Exalted, has done good to him in the religion at the time when he treated Him with ingratitude by his action: in that He made His right greater in his heart than the two abodes, and His prophets and messengers more exalted in his chest than that his self could bear disrespecting a single hair of their hair or underestimating anything of the affairs of the religion of Allah, or inclining to His enemies in which he has chosen and preferred [instead] of the friends of Allah. And all of that is Allah's goodness to him
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument (the 'fourth' in this section). It describes God's favor (iḥsān) upon the believer in the religion, even when the believer acts ungratefully. God has placed in the believer's heart a profound respect: He made His right (ḥaqq) greater than the two abodes (i.e., this world and the Hereafter), and made the prophets and messengers so exalted that the believer's self cannot tolerate disrespecting even a hair of them or underestimating anything of the religion, or inclining to God's enemies. The believer chooses and prefers God's friends. All of this is divine favor. This inherent respect makes the believer worthy of forgiveness, unlike the disbeliever who lacks it.

Key Terms
الإحسان
الدين
حق الله
الأنبياء
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That faith is purely a human achievement and does not involve divine intervention.
Correction: Maturidi argues that faith includes a divinely gifted veneration for God and His prophets, which is an act of divine favor (iḥsān) that makes the believer deserving of forgiveness.

Checkpoint
What does the eighth argument say about the believer that makes him eligible for forgiveness?
A) He never sins
B) He has a natural respect for God and His prophets
C) He repents immediately
D) He does not commit major sins

Key Takeaway
God's favor includes instilling in the believer a profound veneration for Himself and His prophets, which is a divine gift that makes forgiveness possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says Allah has made His right greater in your heart than both this world and the next. Is that genuinely true of you right now? If not, what specific worldly or otherworldly desire competes with that right, and what will you do today to dethrone it?
?
Think of a time you belittled a religious obligation (e.g., a prayer, a command) because of inconvenience. Did you feel the weight of God's right in that moment? What would it take for you to feel that weight so deeply that disrespect becomes unimaginable?
?
When you sin, do you recognize that Allah's favor is still keeping you from outright disrespecting His prophets and religion? How does this realization change the way you view your own ingratitude and your need for His mercy?

Refuting the Mu'tazilah: Unknown Sin Boundary Forces Suspension on Faith Name
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazili unknown sin boundary forces suspension on naming faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili position by showing that their own principle of an unknown boundary between minor and major sins forces them to suspend judgment on naming anyone a believer.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilah's own unknown boundary between sins forces them to suspend naming faith.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful internal critique of the Mu'tazili school. He argues that their doctrine of an unknown boundary between minor and major sins—designed to keep believers in a state of fear and hope—actually forces them to suspend judgment on whether someone is a believer at all. This passage exposes the inconsistency at the heart of the Mu'tazili position on naming faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the flow of Maturidi's argument.
The passage ends abruptly; consider what conclusion might follow.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili principle
Notice that Maturidi starts by stating the Mu'tazili claim: the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown.
3
Follow the logical consequence
Maturidi argues that since no one knows where the boundary is, doubt arises about whether a sin is major (removing faith) or minor (not removing faith). This doubt prevents the Mu'tazilah from confidently naming anyone a believer.
4
Observe the inconsistency
Maturidi points out that the same doubt that prevents security/despair also prevents naming faith, yet the Mu'tazilah allow naming faith with fear. This is inconsistent.
5
Consider the abrupt ending
The passage ends with 'and Allah has informed you,' possibly leading to a conclusion. Reflect on what that conclusion might be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما يُلزم المعتزلة على مذهبهم الوقف في التسمى بالإيمان. وهو أن مذهبهم أن الحد الذي بين الصغيرة والكبيرة من المعاصي غير معروف، ليكون المرء خائفاً راجيا لا آمنا آيسا. فنقول: إذ لا أحد منكم يدعى براءة نفسه من كل، ولا العلم ببلوغ الحد الذي يوجب الأمن أو الإياس، فذلك تردد الحال بين الكبيرة والصغيرة والكبيرة تزيل اسم الإيمان، والصغيرة لحقكم الشك [بها] في اسم الإيمان وزواله كما لحقكم في اسم الكبيرة والصغيرة؛ فإذا منع ذلك الشك القول بالأمن أو الإياس. ثم الذي منع التسمى بالإيمان والذي يدفع هذين واحد ثم لما جاز إثبات الاسم مع الخوف، وأخبركم [الله]
Then we mention a portion of what obliges the Mu'tazilah, according to their school, to suspend judgment (al-waqf) regarding the naming of faith (al-tasammi bi al-iman). And it is that their school is that the boundary between minor sin (saghira) and major sin (kabira) among acts of disobedience is not known, so that a person may be fearful (kha'if) and hopeful (raji), not secure (amin) nor despairing (ya'is). So we say: Since none of you claims innocence of his soul from everything, nor knowledge of reaching the boundary that necessitates security or despair, so that is a fluctuation of the state between the major and minor, and the major removes the name of faith (iman), and regarding the minor, doubt has befallen you [about] the name of faith and its removal just as it has befallen you regarding the name of major and minor. So if that doubt prevents the statement of security or despair, then what prevents the naming of faith and what repels these two is one. Then since it is permissible to affirm the name with fear, and [Allah] has informed you...
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili position on naming faith. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazilah are forced to suspend judgment (waqf) regarding calling someone a believer because of their own principle: the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, so that a person remains between fear and hope. The author argues: Since no one can claim complete innocence or knowledge that they have reached the boundary of security or despair, their state is uncertain between major and minor sin. A major sin removes the name of faith, and a minor sin leaves doubt as to whether faith remains. This doubt prevents them from saying they are secure or despairing. Then the same factor that prevents naming faith also repels both security and despair. Since it is permissible to affirm the name 'faith' along with fear (khawf) — as the Mu'tazilah themselves allow — the argument shows inconsistency. The text ends abruptly with 'and Allah has informed you', possibly leading to a conclusion that is lost in the manuscript.

Key Terms
وقف (suspension)
تسمى بالإيمان (naming of faith)
صغيرة (minor sin)
كبيرة (major sin)
خوف (fear)
أمن (security)
إياس (despair)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilah are consistent in their position on naming faith and the unknown sin boundary.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their own principle forces them to suspend judgment on naming faith, contradicting their allowance of naming faith with fear. The very reason they give for not claiming security/despair also applies to naming faith, so they must also suspend there.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue the Mu'tazilah are forced to do regarding the name 'faith'?
A) Affirm it confidently.
B) Deny it completely.
C) Suspend judgment (waqf).
D) Follow the Ash'ari position.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazili doctrine of an unknown sin boundary logically forces them to suspend judgment on calling anyone a believer, revealing an internal inconsistency in their position on naming faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume you are a believer simply because you haven't committed what you consider a 'major' sin? How does the unknown boundary between sins challenge that assumption and call you to constant self-examination?
?
When you judge others' faith based on their apparent sins, are you not falling into the same trap as the Mu'tazilah? Will you now suspend judgment and leave their state to Allah?
?
If the boundary between sins is unknown, how can you ever feel secure in your faith? What does this lesson teach you about maintaining a balance of fear and hope without despairing?

Eternal Punishment is Only for Shirk
Maturidi proves that eternal threat applies only to shirk using human nature, intellect, and revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the threat of eternal punishment applies exclusively to shirk, based on human nature and intellect.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment is reserved for shirk alone, as human nature and reason confirm.

Maturidi presents a fascinating argument from human nature and intellect to show that eternal punishment cannot apply to minor sins. He observes that people instinctively recoil from leaving their religion, even if it is false, and this natural aversion points to the unique status of shirk. The passage also critiques the Mu'tazilite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice that Maturidi uses the term 'jibilla' (innate nature) as a key concept.
2
Identify the evidence from human nature
Maturidi argues that all people, regardless of their religion, have an innate aversion to leaving their religion. This shows that eternal punishment for leaving religion (shirk) is natural.
Even those who follow false religions cling to them, so how much more for true belief?
3
Understand the intellectual argument
Maturidi points out that beliefs are considered eternal by their adherents, while actions are changeable. This supports the idea that eternal punishment attaches to belief (shirk) rather than actions.
The intellect naturally sees beliefs as more fundamental.
4
Consider the support from revelation
Revealed texts (samʿ) also vary in their rulings on actions, but the threat of eternity is constant for shirk.
The passage references that actions are pointed to as variable.
5
Recognize the critique of Muʿtazila
Maturidi concludes that the Muʿtazilite position (that major sins incur eternal punishment) goes against both human nature and rational governance.
This helps to show the soundness of the Sunni view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدلالة على وعيد الخلود بأنه لا يحتمله ما دون الشرك الأمر الذي جبل عليه الخلق من نفارهم عما به الخروج من أديانهم التي اعتقدوها، وإن كانوا اعتقدوها عقلاً أو حجة أو تقليدا، على وجود ما دون ذلك من الزلات فيهم؛ وإن اختلفت أديانهم. فدل أن ذا مما جبل عليه الخلق؛ بل أيد ذلك العقول، إذ الاعتقادات تكون عند أربابها أبديات، ولا كذلك الأفعال التي تشار إليها، وعلى ذلك أضدادها. وكذلك السمع في الأفعال المشارة أنها على الاختلاف، فعلى ذلك تركها. فدل ما ذكرنا على خروج مذهب الاعتزال عن الأمر المجبول عليه، والمدفوع إليه أيضاً بالتدبير. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the evidence for the threat of eternity (waʿīd al-khulūd) is that what is less than shirk cannot bear it, the matter upon which creation is naturally disposed (jubila ʿalayhi) from their aversion to what takes them out of their religions which they believed, even if they believed them by reason or proof or imitation, despite the existence of lapses less than that among them; and even if their religions differ. So this indicates that this is something upon which creation is naturally disposed; rather, the intellects support that, since beliefs are eternal in the view of their adherents, and actions that are pointed to are not so, and likewise their opposites. And similarly, the revealed texts (al-samʿ) concerning actions that are pointed to are upon difference, so upon that they abandon them. So what we have mentioned indicates the departure of the Muʿtazilite school from the matter upon which one is naturally disposed, and also from what is driven to by governance (al-tadbīr). And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage provides evidence that the threat of eternal punishment (waʿīd al-khulūd) applies only to shirk, not to lesser sins. Maturidi argues that human nature (jibilla) inherently recoils from anything that would drive them out of the religion they have embraced, even if that religion is based on reason, proof, or tradition. This aversion exists despite the presence of minor lapses. He points out that beliefs are considered eternal by their adherents, whereas actions are not; similarly, revealed texts vary regarding actions. Therefore, the Muʿtazila position that major sins incur eternal punishment goes against both innate human nature and the implications of divine governance.

Key Terms
وعيد
خلود
شرك
جبل
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All major sins lead to eternal punishment in Hell.
Correction: Maturidi argues that only shirk incurs eternal punishment, not minor sins, based on human nature, intellect, and revelation.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim supports the idea that eternal punishment is only for shirk?
A) The consensus of scholars
B) Human innate nature and intellect
C) The sayings of the Companions
D) The literal meaning of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument from human nature and intellect shows that eternal punishment is uniquely reserved for shirk, not for lesser sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that only shirk carries eternal punishment, or do you secretly fear that your other sins might doom you forever? How does this fear affect your relationship with Allah, and what would it take for you to fully trust His promise about lesser sins?
?
If even people of false religions cling to their beliefs out of natural disposition, how deep is your own conviction in Islam? Is it based on evidence and certainty, or are you merely following what your family and society have taught you? Examine your heart: what would you be willing to abandon your faith for?
?
Maturidi argues that beliefs are eternal in the eyes of their holders. Have you ever considered your own beliefs as eternal and unchangeable? When you sin, do you see it as a temporary lapse or as a reflection of an eternal truth about your identity? How does this perspective shape your repentance and your reliance on Allah's mercy?

The Unknown Boundary Between Sins and the Waqf of Calling Oneself a Believer
Maturidi identifies the unknown boundary as a reason for waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's own position on the unknown boundary between minor and major sins logically forces them to suspend calling themselves believers.

Core Idea
Uncertainty about one's sins prevents certainty about one's faith.

This lesson begins with Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazila. He first explains their stance that the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, which is meant to keep a person in a state of fear and hope. Then he shows that this very uncertainty makes it impossible for them to confidently call themselves believers.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila stance
Read the passage to grasp the Mu'tazila view that the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, to maintain fear and hope.
2
Follow Maturidi's argument
Notice how Maturidi argues that since no one can claim innocence or knowledge of the boundary, the situation is uncertain. A major sin removes faith; a minor sin causes doubt.
3
Realize the consequence
Understand that the same uncertainty that prevents claiming security or despair also forces the Mu'tazila to stop (waqf) calling themselves believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما يُلزم المعتزلة على مذهبهم الوقف في التسمى بالإيمان. وهو أن مذهبهم أن الحد الذي بين الصغيرة والكبيرة من المعاصي غير معروف، ليكون المرء خائفاً راجيا لا آمنا آيسا. فنقول: إذ لا أحد منكم يدعي براءة نفسه من كل، ولا العلم ببلوغ الحد الذي يوجب الأمن أو الإياس، فذلك تردد الحال بين الكبيرة والصغيرة والكبيرة تزيل اسم الإيمان، والصغيرة لحقكم الشك بها في اسم الإيمان وزواله كما لحقكم في اسم الكبيرة والصغيرة؛ فإذا منع ذلك الشك القول بالأمن أو الإياس. ثم الذي منع التسمى بالإيمان والذي يدفع هذين واحد. ثم لما جاز إثبات الاسم مع الخوف، وأخبركم الله أن المؤمن لا خوف عليه، لم لا تخافون من تسميتكم أنفسكم مؤمنين؟ الكذب الذي لعله كبيرة يزيل عنكم اسم الإيمان، فيكون التسمية من كبر أنفسكم، وقد حذرتم عن تزكية الأنفس بقوله تعالى: {فلا تزكوا أنفسكم} [النجم، 32]. ثم تعارضون بالبر والتقوى أتشهدون أنفسكم بهما أو لا؟ فإن شهدوا لزمهم بالخوف في المتقين الأبرار أن يكونوا استوجبوا مقت الله والخلود في النار فيكون جهنم دار المتقين الأبرار لا دار الفاسقين؛ وقد قال الله تعالى: {وإن الأبرار لفي نعيم} [الانفطار، 13] الآية، ويبطل الدعاء بقوله: {وتوفنا مع الأبرار} [آل عمران، 193]. وإن أبوا التسمية بذلك لزمهم مثله في الإيمان، إذ هو اسم لما مقت الله كالبر والتقوى. ثم يقال: إنه قد ثبت عن الأنبياء والرسل أنهم كانوا يدعون الله رغباً ورهباً وخوفاً وطمعاً ويزعمون أنهم لم يبتلوا بكبائر، فقد كان هذا الخوف ينل بها، لم لا دلكم أن ليس ترك بيان الحد لما يخاف ويرجى، بل ذلك لما الله معاقبة شاء بالصغائر؟ ومن قولكم إن ما يوجب العقوبة يزيل اسم الإيمان، فاعتبروا بأن لستم مؤمنين على ما أخبرتم في الحقيقة. والله الموفق.
Then we mention a portion of what compels the Muʿtazila according to their school to stop (al-waqf) in calling oneself a believer. And it is that their school is that the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, so that a person may be fearing and hoping, not secure and despairing. So we say: Since none of you claims innocence of everything, nor knowledge of reaching the boundary that necessitates security or despair, so the situation wavers between major and minor sins. The major sin removes the name of faith, and the minor sin, you are uncertain about the name of faith and its removal, just as you are uncertain about the name of major and minor. So that uncertainty prevents the statement of security or despair. Then what prevents calling oneself a believer and what repels these two is one. Then when it is permissible to affirm the name with fear, and Allah informed you that the believer has no fear, why do you not fear that naming yourselves believers might be a lie which is perhaps a major sin that removes from you the name of faith? So the naming would be from the pride of yourselves, and you have been warned against self-praise by His saying: 'Do not praise yourselves' [Quran 53:32]. Then you contradict yourselves regarding righteousness (al-birr) and piety (al-taqwā): do you testify for yourselves with them or not? If they testify, then fear would necessitate that the righteous and pious have merited Allah's wrath and eternity in the Fire, making Hell the abode of the righteous and pious, not the abode of the wicked. And Allah has said: 'Indeed the righteous are in bliss' [Quran 82:13] and the supplication 'Cause us to die with the righteous' [Quran 3:193] would be invalid. And if they refuse to name themselves with that, the same applies to faith, since it is a name for what Allah dislikes like righteousness and piety. Then it is said: It is established from the prophets and messengers that they used to call upon Allah with desire and awe, fear and hope, and they claim they were not afflicted with major sins. So this fear was due to them. Why does this not indicate to you that leaving the clarification of the boundary for what is feared and hoped is not the reason, but rather it is because Allah punishes with minor sins as He wills? And from your statement that what necessitates punishment removes the name of faith, so consider that you are not believers in reality, as you have informed. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazilite position regarding calling oneself a believer. Maturidi first states that the Muʿtazila hold that the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, so a person remains in fear and hope. He then argues that since no one can claim innocence or knowledge of the exact boundary, the situation is uncertain. A major sin removes the name of faith, and a minor sin causes doubt about the name. This doubt prevents any claim of security or despair. Maturidi points out that the same logic requires them to stop calling themselves believers. He then raises an objection: if the name of believer can be affirmed with fear, and Allah says the believer has no fear, then calling oneself a believer might be a lie and a major sin. He also uses the examples of righteousness and piety: if the Muʿtazila testify to being righteous and pious, that would imply that the righteous deserve Hell, contradicting Quranic verses. If they refuse, the same applies to faith. Finally, he notes that prophets themselves feared God despite being free from major sins, showing that fear does not indicate uncertainty about faith status, but rather punishment for minor sins is possible. The conclusion: the Muʿtazila's own position forces them to admit they are not believers in reality.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf)
تسمى (tasammī)
إيمان (īmān)
كبيرة (kabīra)
صغيرة (saghīra)
خوف (khawf)
أمن (amn)
يأس (ya's)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what is the main reason the Mu'tazila must stop calling themselves believers?
A) They admit to committing major sins.
B) The boundary between minor and major sins is unknown.
C) Quran prohibits self-praise.
D) They cannot be sure of being righteous.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's own principle of an unknown boundary between sins logically requires them to suspend calling themselves believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you do not know the exact boundary between minor and major sins, how can you be certain that you are still a believer? Will you examine your own sins today with the same fear and hope this passage demands?
?
Are you more concerned with labeling yourself a 'believer' or with the state of your actions before Allah? What would it take for you to stop seeking comfort in a label and start fearing the uncertainty of your deeds?
?
This passage forces the Mu‘tazila to stop calling themselves believers. Have you ever truly doubted your own faith because of your sins? If not, are you living in false security? Let this uncertainty drive you to sincere repentance today.

The Risk of Lying and Self-Praise in Calling Oneself a Believer
Maturidi argues claiming faith risks lying and self-praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that calling oneself a believer while uncertain of one's state may constitute a lie and thus a major sin, and that such self-praise is prohibited in the Quran.

Core Idea
Claiming faith while uncertain can be a sinful lie.

Maturidi continues his refutation by pointing out a dangerous consequence: if the Mu'tazila call themselves believers despite uncertainty, they might be lying. A lie could be a major sin, which would remove the very name they claim. Moreover, such self-praise is prohibited in the Quran. This further forces them to stop.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument
Read the passage to see how Maturidi asks: if faith is claimed with fear, why are you not afraid that claim might be a lie?
2
Recognize the risk
Realize that a lie could be a major sin, which removes the name of faith, so such a claim could destroy the very thing you claim.
3
See the Quranic prohibition
Maturidi quotes Quran 53:32 forbidding self-praise, showing that calling yourself a believer without certainty is a form of tazkiyat al-nafs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر طرفًا مما يُلزم المعتزلة على مذهبهم الوقف في التسمى بالإيمان. وهو أن مذهبهم أن الحد الذي بين الصغيرة والكبيرة من المعاصي غير معروف، ليكون المرء خائفاً راجيا لا آمنا آيسا. فنقول: إذ لا أحد منكم يدعي براءة نفسه من كل، ولا العلم ببلوغ الحد الذي يوجب الأمن أو الإياس، فذلك تردد الحال بين الكبيرة والصغيرة والكبيرة تزيل اسم الإيمان، والصغيرة لحقكم الشك بها في اسم الإيمان وزواله كما لحقكم في اسم الكبيرة والصغيرة؛ فإذا منع ذلك الشك القول بالأمن أو الإياس. ثم الذي منع التسمى بالإيمان والذي يدفع هذين واحد. ثم لما جاز إثبات الاسم مع الخوف، وأخبركم الله أن المؤمن لا خوف عليه، لم لا تخافون من تسميتكم أنفسكم مؤمنين؟ الكذب الذي لعله كبيرة يزيل عنكم اسم الإيمان، فيكون التسمية من كبر أنفسكم، وقد حذرتم عن تزكية الأنفس بقوله تعالى: {فلا تزكوا أنفسكم} [النجم، 32]. ثم تعارضون بالبر والتقوى أتشهدون أنفسكم بهما أو لا؟ فإن شهدوا لزمهم بالخوف في المتقين الأبرار أن يكونوا استوجبوا مقت الله والخلود في النار فيكون جهنم دار المتقين الأبرار لا دار الفاسقين؛ وقد قال الله تعالى: {وإن الأبرار لفي نعيم} [الانفطار، 13] الآية، ويبطل الدعاء بقوله: {وتوفنا مع الأبرار} [آل عمران، 193]. وإن أبوا التسمية بذلك لزمهم مثله في الإيمان، إذ هو اسم لما مقت الله كالبر والتقوى. ثم يقال: إنه قد ثبت عن الأنبياء والرسل أنهم كانوا يدعون الله رغباً ورهباً وخوفاً وطمعاً ويزعمون أنهم لم يبتلوا بكبائر، فقد كان هذا الخوف ينل بها، لم لا دلكم أن ليس ترك بيان الحد لما يخاف ويرجى، بل ذلك لما الله معاقبة شاء بالصغائر؟ ومن قولكم إن ما يوجب العقوبة يزيل اسم الإيمان، فاعتبروا بأن لستم مؤمنين على ما أخبرتم في الحقيقة. والله الموفق.
Then we mention a portion of what compels the Muʿtazila according to their school to stop (al-waqf) in calling oneself a believer. And it is that their school is that the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, so that a person may be fearing and hoping, not secure and despairing. So we say: Since none of you claims innocence of everything, nor knowledge of reaching the boundary that necessitates security or despair, so the situation wavers between major and minor sins. The major sin removes the name of faith, and the minor sin, you are uncertain about the name of faith and its removal, just as you are uncertain about the name of major and minor. So that uncertainty prevents the statement of security or despair. Then what prevents calling oneself a believer and what repels these two is one. Then when it is permissible to affirm the name with fear, and Allah informed you that the believer has no fear, why do you not fear that naming yourselves believers might be a lie which is perhaps a major sin that removes from you the name of faith? So the naming would be from the pride of yourselves, and you have been warned against self-praise by His saying: 'Do not praise yourselves' [Quran 53:32]. Then you contradict yourselves regarding righteousness (al-birr) and piety (al-taqwā): do you testify for yourselves with them or not? If they testify, then fear would necessitate that the righteous and pious have merited Allah's wrath and eternity in the Fire, making Hell the abode of the righteous and pious, not the abode of the wicked. And Allah has said: 'Indeed the righteous are in bliss' [Quran 82:13] and the supplication 'Cause us to die with the righteous' [Quran 3:193] would be invalid. And if they refuse to name themselves with that, the same applies to faith, since it is a name for what Allah dislikes like righteousness and piety. Then it is said: It is established from the prophets and messengers that they used to call upon Allah with desire and awe, fear and hope, and they claim they were not afflicted with major sins. So this fear was due to them. Why does this not indicate to you that leaving the clarification of the boundary for what is feared and hoped is not the reason, but rather it is because Allah punishes with minor sins as He wills? And from your statement that what necessitates punishment removes the name of faith, so consider that you are not believers in reality, as you have informed. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Muʿtazilite position regarding calling oneself a believer. Maturidi first states that the Muʿtazila hold that the boundary between minor and major sins is unknown, so a person remains in fear and hope. He then argues that since no one can claim innocence or knowledge of the exact boundary, the situation is uncertain. A major sin removes the name of faith, and a minor sin causes doubt about the name. This doubt prevents any claim of security or despair. Maturidi points out that the same logic requires them to stop calling themselves believers. He then raises an objection: if the name of believer can be affirmed with fear, and Allah says the believer has no fear, then calling oneself a believer might be a lie and a major sin. He also uses the examples of righteousness and piety: if the Muʿtazila testify to being righteous and pious, that would imply that the righteous deserve Hell, contradicting Quranic verses. If they refuse, the same applies to faith. Finally, he notes that prophets themselves feared God despite being free from major sins, showing that fear does not indicate uncertainty about faith status, but rather punishment for minor sins is possible. The conclusion: the Muʿtazila's own position forces them to admit they are not believers in reality.

Key Terms
كذب (kidhb)
تزكية (tazkiya)
كبر (kibr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Claiming to be a believer is always a praiseworthy act of faith.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if done without certainty, it may be a lie and a major sin, and it is a form of self-praise prohibited in the Quran.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is calling oneself a believer potentially a major sin?
A) It shows arrogance.
B) It might be a lie because one is not certain of one's faith.
C) It involves praising oneself, which is prohibited.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Calling oneself a believer while uncertain risks being a sinful lie and an act of self-praise, both of which contradict faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer,' do you ever pause to honestly examine whether your certainty and actions match that claim, or do you rely on the label without self-reflection? What would it mean for you if that claim turned out to be a lie?
?
The Quran says 'Do not praise yourselves' (53:32). How often do you find yourself boasting about your faith, knowledge, or piety to others? Could Maturidi's warning about self-praise apply to you?
?
If Allah asked you right now, 'Are you certain that you are a true believer?' what would your honest answer be? How does that uncertainty make you feel, and what will you do about it today?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The Contradiction: Righteousness, Piety, and Faith
Maturidi uses a dilemma regarding righteousness and piety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's use of a contradiction argument: the Mu'tazila's treatment of righteousness (birr) and piety (taqwa) forces them to apply the same logic to faith.

Core Idea
If claiming righteousness leads to absurdity, so does claiming faith.

Maturidi now employs a powerful reductio ad absurdum. He asks the Mu'tazila whether they would claim to be righteous and pious. If they do, that would imply the righteous deserve Hell, contradicting the Quran. If they refuse, the same logic applies to faith. This shows the inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the passage to see the question: do the Mu'tazila testify to themselves as righteous and pious?
2
Consequence of 'yes'
If they claim righteousness, their own logic would force them to say the righteous deserve Hell, contradicting Quran 82:13 and 3:193.
3
Consequence of 'no'
If they refuse to claim righteousness, the same uncertainty must apply to faith, forcing them to stop calling themselves believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception: Righteousness and piety are different from faith in terms of certainty.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue would happen if the Mu'tazila claim to be righteous and pious?
A) They would be correct.
B) It would contradict the Quranic promise of bliss for the righteous.
C) They would be safe from Hell.
D) They would be forced to admit their faith is secure.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently claim righteousness without absurdity, and they cannot refuse without denying themselves faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever refer to yourself as 'just a believer' to avoid the weight of being called 'righteous' or 'pious'? How does Maturidi's dilemma reveal that your hesitation may be a form of double standards—claiming faith without fully embracing its implications? What would it cost you to sincerely call yourself a believer today without hedging?
?
If you applied the same uncertainty to your righteousness that the Mu'tazila applied to faith, would you fear Allah's wrath? Where in your life do you let fear of hypocrisy keep you from fully claiming what Allah has named you? What would it mean to trust His promise that the righteous are in bliss, even when you see your own shortcomings?
?
Maturidi argues that refusing to call yourself righteous leads to refusing faith itself. When you say 'I am a believer,' do you mean it with the same certainty as you would if you said 'I am a parent' or 'I am a citizen'? If not, what is holding you back from that level of conviction? How can you move from theoretical faith to a self-testimony that risks being genuine?

The Prophetic Example of Fear and the Final Refutation
Maturidi uses prophetic fear to refute the Mu'tazila and conclude they are not believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the example of prophets who feared Allah despite being free of major sins to argue that fear does not indicate uncertainty about faith, and that his final conclusion is that the Mu'tazila's own principles force them to admit they are not believers.

Core Idea
Prophets feared despite sinlessness, showing fear is not about uncertainty but about possible punishment for minor sins.

Maturidi's final argument appeals to the example of the prophets and messengers. They called upon Allah with fear and hope, yet they were free from major sins. This shows that fear does not stem from uncertainty about the boundary between sins; rather, it stems from the possibility that Allah may punish for minor sins. This undercuts the Mu'tazila's entire framework and leads to the conclusion that they cannot truly call themselves believers.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the prophetic example
Read the passage noting that prophets called upon Allah with fear and hope, yet were sinless of major sins.
2
Understand the implication
This shows that fear is not due to uncertainty about the sin boundary, but due to the possibility of punishment for minor sins.
3
See the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that since the Mu'tazila cannot be certain they are free from sins that necessitate punishment, and such punishment removes faith, they are not believers in reality.

According to Maturidi, what does the example of the prophets demonstrate?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live with a genuine fear that your minor sins could have consequences in this life or the next, or do you take them lightly? What would change if you truly believed they matter to Allah?
?

?
The prophets called upon Allah with both fear and hope. Which of these two is weaker in your heart right now, and what concrete step will you take this week to restore balance?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
The Contradiction in Mu'tazila on Fear and Faith
Maturidi points out a contradiction in Mu'tazila regarding fear and faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contradiction Maturidi identifies in the Mu'tazila position regarding fear and faith.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila's own claims about faith and fear contradict each other.

Maturidi uses a Quranic verse to expose a logical flaw in Mu'tazila theology. They claim believers neither fear nor hope, yet they oblige fear. This lesson examines that contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once. Notice the Quranic verse and the logical flow of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claims
From the passage, extract what Maturidi says about the Mu'tazila position: what do they believe about the believer's fear, hope, and merit?
Look for phrases like 'the believer does not fear' and 'has already merited mercy'.
3
Understand the contradiction
Maturidi asks how they can oblige fear while denying it in reality. Reflect on why this is a contradiction and what it reveals about Mu'tazila inconsistency.
This step directly addresses the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال الله تعالى: {إنما المؤمنون الذين آمنوا بالله ورسوله ثم لم يرتابوا} [الحجرات، 15] وعندكم المؤمن لا يخاف نقمة الله ولا يرجو رحمته بل قد استوجب رحمته لو كان مؤمنا، وليس الله أن يعذبه لو كان مؤمنًا، والإيمان هو الذي حملهم على ذلك. فكيف ألزمتموهم الخوف وذلك ليس على المؤمن في الحقيقة ومنعتموهم على الارتياب في الإيمان، والارتياب فيه بما رأى الخوف؟ وذلك بين التناقض. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And Allah the Exalted has said: 'Indeed the believers are those who believe in Allah and His Messenger and then do not doubt' [Quran 49:15]. And according to you, the believer does not fear Allah's punishment nor hope for His mercy, but rather he has already merited His mercy if he were a believer, and it is not for Allah to punish him if he were a believer, and faith is what carried them to that. So how have you obliged them to fear—and that is not upon the believer in reality—and prevented them from doubt about faith, and doubt about it because of the fear they see? And that is clear contradiction. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage highlights a contradiction in the Muʿtazilite position. Maturidi cites Quran 49:15 which describes believers as those who do not doubt. He then notes that according to the Muʿtazila, a believer does not fear Allah's punishment nor hope for mercy, but rather has already merited mercy. If that is the case, Maturidi asks, how can they require believers to fear (as in their doctrine of the intermediate position) while also claiming that believers in reality do not fear? Moreover, they prevent doubt about faith, yet fear would imply doubt. This is a clear contradiction.

Key Terms
ارتياب (irtiyāb)
خوف (khawf)
إيمان (īmān)
تناقض (tanāquḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that fear and faith are compatible without contradiction, but Maturidi shows the Mu'tazila's own principles lead to inconsistency.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that if believers are truly secure (as Mu'tazila claim), then requiring fear is contradictory because fear implies possible punishment, which contradicts security.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Maturidi point out in the Mu'tazila position?
A) They say believers merit mercy yet require them to fear
B) They say believers have no fear yet require them to hope
C) They say doubt is prohibited yet require belief in uncertainty
D) They say major sins remove faith yet allow hope for forgiveness

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position that believers merit mercy contradicts their requirement of fear, as Maturidi demonstrates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live with the certainty that your faith guarantees paradise, or do you fear Allah's punishment? If both, how do you reconcile them? What does your fear reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
The Mu'tazila commanded fear even while claiming believers are secure. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah that contradict each other? For example, believing in His mercy but acting as if He will not forgive you. How can you align your heart with the truth?
?
When you sin, does your fear of punishment lead you to doubt your faith? How can you transform that fear into a motivation for repentance without losing certainty in Allah's mercy? Be specific.
